## 21. Competitive Positioning Maps

Competitive positioning maps provide visual representations of how competitors are positioned in the market relative to each other and relative to customer needs. These strategic tools help identify market gaps, competitive clusters, differentiation opportunities, and positioning strategies. This section covers three core positioning map methodologies: perceptual mapping, value curves (strategy canvas), and feature matrices.

### 21.1 Perceptual Mapping

Perceptual maps plot competitors on two-dimensional axes representing key market dimensions. They reveal how customers perceive competitive positions and identify whitespace opportunities.

**Core Methodology:**

**Step 1: Dimension Selection**

Choose two dimensions that are:
- Important to customers (drive purchase decisions)
- Differentiating (competitors vary meaningfully)
- Independent (not correlated with each other)
- Measurable (can be assessed objectively or through research)

**Common Dimension Pairs by Industry:**

**B2B SaaS:**
- Price vs. Features (richness)
- Ease of Use vs. Power/Sophistication
- Specialization vs. Generalization
- Enterprise Focus vs. SMB Focus
- Innovation vs. Stability
- Self-Service vs. White-Glove Service

**E-commerce:**
- Price vs. Quality
- Selection (breadth) vs. Curation (depth)
- Mass Market vs. Niche
- Fast Shipping vs. Low Cost
- Branded vs. Generic/Private Label

**Consumer Apps:**
- Simple vs. Feature-Rich
- Privacy-Focused vs. Social/Connected
- Free vs. Premium
- Mainstream vs. Technical Users
- Entertainment vs. Productivity

**Financial Services:**
- Traditional vs. Digital-First
- Full Service vs. Self-Directed
- Low Cost vs. Premium Service
- Conservative vs. Innovative

**Step 2: Data Collection for Positioning**

Gather positioning data through multiple methods:

**Method A: Customer Perception Survey**

Design a survey asking customers to rate competitors on each dimension:

```
Survey Structure:

Instructions: "Please rate each company on the following dimensions using a 1-10 scale."

Dimension 1: Ease of Use
1 (Very Difficult) -------- 5 (Moderate) -------- 10 (Very Easy)

[Company A]: [Rating 1-10]
[Company B]: [Rating 1-10]
[Your Company]: [Rating 1-10]
[Company C]: [Rating 1-10]

Dimension 2: Features/Capabilities
1 (Basic Features) -------- 5 (Moderate) -------- 10 (Extremely Comprehensive)

[Company A]: [Rating 1-10]
[Company B]: [Rating 1-10]
[Your Company]: [Rating 1-10]
[Company C]: [Rating 1-10]

Demographics:
- Company size: [...]
- Industry: [...]
- Familiarity with each company: [Not familiar / Somewhat / Very familiar]
```

**Sample Size Requirements:**
- Minimum 50 responses for directional insights
- 200+ responses for statistical reliability
- 500+ responses for segment-level analysis

**Method B: Review Mining**

Analyze product reviews (G2, Capterra, TrustRadius) to infer positioning:

```python
# Python script to extract positioning from reviews

import pandas as pd
import re
from collections import Counter

def extract_positioning_signals(reviews_df, dimension_keywords):
    """
    Extract positioning scores from review text
    
    Args:
        reviews_df: DataFrame with columns ['company', 'review_text', 'rating']
        dimension_keywords: Dict mapping dimensions to keyword lists
        
    Returns:
        DataFrame with positioning scores by company and dimension
    """
    
    results = []
    
    for company in reviews_df['company'].unique():
        company_reviews = reviews_df[reviews_df['company'] == company]
        company_scores = {'company': company}
        
        for dimension, keywords in dimension_keywords.items():
            # Count positive mentions
            positive_count = 0
            negative_count = 0
            total_mentions = 0
            
            for review in company_reviews['review_text']:
                review_lower = review.lower()
                
                # Check for dimension mentions
                mentions = sum([review_lower.count(kw.lower()) for kw in keywords])
                
                if mentions > 0:
                    total_mentions += mentions
                    
                    # Sentiment analysis (simple keyword approach)
                    positive_words = ['easy', 'simple', 'intuitive', 'great', 
                                    'excellent', 'powerful', 'comprehensive']
                    negative_words = ['difficult', 'complex', 'confusing', 
                                    'limited', 'lacking', 'poor', 'weak']
                    
                    pos_score = sum([review_lower.count(w) for w in positive_words])
                    neg_score = sum([review_lower.count(w) for w in negative_words])
                    
                    if pos_score > neg_score:
                        positive_count += mentions
                    else:
                        negative_count += mentions
            
            # Calculate dimension score (1-10 scale)
            if total_mentions > 0:
                sentiment_ratio = positive_count / (positive_count + negative_count)
                dimension_score = 1 + (sentiment_ratio * 9)  # Scale to 1-10
            else:
                dimension_score = 5  # Neutral if no mentions
                
            company_scores[dimension] = round(dimension_score, 1)
            company_scores[f'{dimension}_mentions'] = total_mentions
        
        results.append(company_scores)
    
    return pd.DataFrame(results)

# Example usage:
dimension_keywords = {
    'ease_of_use': ['easy', 'simple', 'intuitive', 'user-friendly', 
                    'straightforward', 'quick to learn'],
    'features': ['features', 'capabilities', 'functionality', 
                'comprehensive', 'powerful', 'advanced'],
    'support': ['support', 'customer service', 'help', 'responsive',
               'documentation', 'training'],
    'value': ['price', 'value', 'worth', 'affordable', 'expensive',
             'cost', 'pricing']
}

# Load review data (from G2, Capterra exports or API)
reviews = pd.read_csv('competitor_reviews.csv')

# Extract positioning
positioning = extract_positioning_signals(reviews, dimension_keywords)
print(positioning)
```

**Method C: Objective Feature Analysis**

For quantifiable dimensions, assess objectively:

**Dimension: Features/Capabilities**
- Count features across competitor products
- Weight by sophistication/value
- Normalize to 1-10 scale

**Dimension: Price**
- Use actual pricing ($ per user per month for SaaS)
- Normalize to 1-10 scale relative to market range

**Dimension: Company Size/Resources**
- Use employee count, funding, revenue
- Normalize to 1-10 scale

Example Python script for objective positioning:

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np

def normalize_to_scale(values, min_scale=1, max_scale=10):
    """Normalize values to scale (default 1-10)"""
    min_val = min(values)
    max_val = max(values)
    
    if max_val == min_val:
        return [5] * len(values)  # All same, return midpoint
    
    normalized = [
        min_scale + ((v - min_val) / (max_val - min_val)) * (max_scale - min_scale)
        for v in values
    ]
    return [round(n, 1) for n in normalized]

# Example: Position competitors on Price vs Features

competitors = {
    'Company A': {'price_per_user': 50, 'feature_count': 120},
    'Company B': {'price_per_user': 30, 'feature_count': 80},
    'Your Company': {'price_per_user': 40, 'feature_count': 100},
    'Company C': {'price_per_user': 70, 'feature_count': 150},
    'Company D': {'price_per_user': 25, 'feature_count': 60},
}

# Extract raw values
companies = list(competitors.keys())
prices = [competitors[c]['price_per_user'] for c in companies]
features = [competitors[c]['feature_count'] for c in companies]

# Normalize (note: inverse for price - lower is better, so reverse scale)
price_scores = normalize_to_scale(prices)
price_scores_inverted = [11 - s for s in price_scores]  # Invert so low price = high score
feature_scores = normalize_to_scale(features)

# Create positioning dataframe
positioning_df = pd.DataFrame({
    'company': companies,
    'price_score': price_scores_inverted,  # Low price = high score
    'feature_score': feature_scores,
    'raw_price': prices,
    'raw_features': features
})

print(positioning_df)
```

**Step 3: Visualization Creation**

Create the perceptual map visualization:

**Python Visualization with Matplotlib:**

```python
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import numpy as np

def create_perceptual_map(positioning_df, x_dim, y_dim, 
                          x_label, y_label, title):
    """
    Create perceptual positioning map
    
    Args:
        positioning_df: DataFrame with company positioning scores
        x_dim: Column name for X-axis dimension
        y_dim: Column name for Y-axis dimension
        x_label: Label for X-axis
        y_label: Label for Y-axis
        title: Chart title
    """
    
    fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(12, 10))
    
    # Set axis limits with padding
    ax.set_xlim(0, 11)
    ax.set_ylim(0, 11)
    
    # Add gridlines
    ax.grid(True, alpha=0.3, linestyle='--')
    
    # Add quadrant lines
    ax.axhline(y=5.5, color='gray', linestyle='-', linewidth=0.8, alpha=0.5)
    ax.axvline(x=5.5, color='gray', linestyle='-', linewidth=0.8, alpha=0.5)
    
    # Plot each competitor
    colors = ['red', 'blue', 'green', 'orange', 'purple', 'brown', 'pink']
    
    for idx, row in positioning_df.iterrows():
        x = row[x_dim]
        y = row[y_dim]
        company = row['company']
        
        # Highlight your company differently
        if 'Your Company' in company or company == positioning_df.iloc[0]['company']:
            color = 'darkblue'
            size = 300
            marker = 's'  # Square
            zorder = 10
        else:
            color = colors[idx % len(colors)]
            size = 200
            marker = 'o'  # Circle
            zorder = 5
        
        # Plot point
        ax.scatter(x, y, s=size, c=color, alpha=0.6, 
                  edgecolors='black', linewidth=1.5, 
                  marker=marker, zorder=zorder)
        
        # Add company label
        ax.annotate(company, (x, y), 
                   xytext=(10, 10), textcoords='offset points',
                   fontsize=10, fontweight='bold',
                   bbox=dict(boxstyle='round,pad=0.3', 
                           facecolor='white', alpha=0.7, edgecolor='black'),
                   zorder=zorder+1)
    
    # Add quadrant labels
    ax.text(2.5, 9, 'Low Price\nHigh Features', 
           ha='center', va='center', fontsize=11, 
           style='italic', alpha=0.6, weight='bold')
    ax.text(8.5, 9, 'High Price\nHigh Features', 
           ha='center', va='center', fontsize=11, 
           style='italic', alpha=0.6, weight='bold')
    ax.text(2.5, 2, 'Low Price\nLow Features', 
           ha='center', va='center', fontsize=11, 
           style='italic', alpha=0.6, weight='bold')
    ax.text(8.5, 2, 'High Price\nLow Features', 
           ha='center', va='center', fontsize=11, 
           style='italic', alpha=0.6, weight='bold')
    
    # Labels and title
    ax.set_xlabel(x_label, fontsize=12, fontweight='bold')
    ax.set_ylabel(y_label, fontsize=12, fontweight='bold')
    ax.set_title(title, fontsize=14, fontweight='bold', pad=20)
    
    # Remove top and right spines
    ax.spines['top'].set_visible(False)
    ax.spines['right'].set_visible(False)
    
    plt.tight_layout()
    
    return fig

# Example usage
fig = create_perceptual_map(
    positioning_df,
    x_dim='price_score',
    y_dim='feature_score',
    x_label='Value (Lower Price) ←→ Premium (Higher Price)',
    y_label='Basic Features ←→ Comprehensive Features',
    title='CRM Software Competitive Positioning Map'
)

plt.savefig('perceptual_map.png', dpi=300, bbox_inches='tight')
plt.show()
```

**Step 4: Strategic Interpretation**

Analyze the perceptual map for strategic insights:

**Quadrant Analysis:**

Depending on axis dimensions, interpret each quadrant's strategic position:

**Example: Price (X) vs. Features (Y)**

**Quadrant 1: Low Price, Low Features (Budget/Entry-Level)**
- Strategy: Cost leadership, volume sales
- Customers: Price-sensitive, basic needs
- Risk: Commoditization, low margins
- Examples: Zoho CRM (free tier), Insightly

**Quadrant 2: Low Price, High Features (Value Leaders)**
- Strategy: Best value proposition, high efficiency
- Customers: Value-conscious, feature-aware
- Advantage: Strong position if sustainable
- Risk: Margin pressure, sustainability questions
- Examples: HubSpot (free + low-cost tiers)

**Quadrant 3: High Price, Low Features (Vulnerable Position)**
- Strategy: Usually untenable unless niche specialization
- Risk: High vulnerability to competition
- Requires: Strong brand, switching costs, or specific niche advantage
- Examples: Legacy players losing ground

**Quadrant 4: High Price, High Features (Premium/Enterprise)**
- Strategy: Premium positioning, enterprise focus
- Customers: Large organizations, complex needs
- Advantage: High margins, strategic accounts
- Risk: Disruption from value leaders
- Examples: Salesforce, Microsoft Dynamics

**Clustering Analysis:**

Identify competitor clusters:

```python
from sklearn.cluster import KMeans
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

def identify_competitive_clusters(positioning_df, x_dim, y_dim, n_clusters=3):
    """
    Use K-means clustering to identify competitive groups
    """
    
    # Prepare data for clustering
    X = positioning_df[[x_dim, y_dim]].values
    
    # Perform clustering
    kmeans = KMeans(n_clusters=n_clusters, random_state=42)
    positioning_df['cluster'] = kmeans.fit_predict(X)
    
    # Visualize clusters
    fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(12, 10))
    
    colors = ['red', 'blue', 'green', 'orange', 'purple']
    
    for cluster_id in range(n_clusters):
        cluster_data = positioning_df[positioning_df['cluster'] == cluster_id]
        
        ax.scatter(cluster_data[x_dim], cluster_data[y_dim],
                  s=200, c=colors[cluster_id], alpha=0.6,
                  label=f'Cluster {cluster_id + 1}',
                  edgecolors='black', linewidth=1.5)
        
        for idx, row in cluster_data.iterrows():
            ax.annotate(row['company'], 
                       (row[x_dim], row[y_dim]),
                       xytext=(10, 10), textcoords='offset points',
                       fontsize=9, bbox=dict(boxstyle='round', 
                       facecolor='white', alpha=0.7))
    
    # Plot cluster centers
    centers = kmeans.cluster_centers_
    ax.scatter(centers[:, 0], centers[:, 1],
              s=500, c='black', marker='X', 
              linewidths=3, label='Cluster Centers',
              zorder=10)
    
    ax.set_xlabel(x_dim, fontsize=12, fontweight='bold')
    ax.set_ylabel(y_dim, fontsize=12, fontweight='bold')
    ax.set_title('Competitive Clustering Analysis', 
                fontsize=14, fontweight='bold')
    ax.legend()
    ax.grid(True, alpha=0.3)
    
    plt.tight_layout()
    return fig, positioning_df

# Example usage
fig, clustered_df = identify_competitive_clusters(
    positioning_df, 
    'price_score', 
    'feature_score',
    n_clusters=3
)

plt.savefig('competitive_clusters.png', dpi=300)
plt.show()

# Analyze clusters
for cluster_id in clustered_df['cluster'].unique():
    cluster_companies = clustered_df[
        clustered_df['cluster'] == cluster_id
    ]['company'].tolist()
    print(f"\nCluster {cluster_id + 1}: {', '.join(cluster_companies)}")
```

**Whitespace Identification:**

Find market gaps where no competitors are positioned:

```python
def identify_whitespace(positioning_df, x_dim, y_dim, grid_size=10):
    """
    Identify whitespace (unoccupied market positions)
    """
    
    # Create grid
    x_bins = np.linspace(1, 10, grid_size)
    y_bins = np.linspace(1, 10, grid_size)
    
    # Create 2D histogram of competitor positions
    occupied, x_edges, y_edges = np.histogram2d(
        positioning_df[x_dim],
        positioning_df[y_dim],
        bins=[x_bins, y_bins]
    )
    
    # Find empty cells (whitespace)
    whitespace_cells = []
    for i in range(len(x_bins)-1):
        for j in range(len(y_bins)-1):
            if occupied[i, j] == 0:  # Unoccupied
                x_center = (x_bins[i] + x_bins[i+1]) / 2
                y_center = (y_bins[j] + y_bins[j+1]) / 2
                whitespace_cells.append({
                    'x': x_center,
                    'y': y_center,
                    'x_range': (x_bins[i], x_bins[i+1]),
                    'y_range': (y_bins[j], y_bins[j+1])
                })
    
    # Visualize whitespace
    fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(12, 10))
    
    # Plot heatmap of competition density
    im = ax.imshow(occupied.T, origin='lower', extent=[1, 10, 1, 10],
                   cmap='YlOrRd', alpha=0.3)
    plt.colorbar(im, ax=ax, label='Number of Competitors')
    
    # Plot competitors
    ax.scatter(positioning_df[x_dim], positioning_df[y_dim],
              s=200, c='blue', edgecolors='black', linewidth=2,
              zorder=5, label='Competitors')
    
    for idx, row in positioning_df.iterrows():
        ax.annotate(row['company'],
                   (row[x_dim], row[y_dim]),
                   xytext=(5, 5), textcoords='offset points',
                   fontsize=8)
    
    # Highlight whitespace
    for cell in whitespace_cells:
        ax.add_patch(plt.Rectangle(
            (cell['x_range'][0], cell['y_range'][0]),
            cell['x_range'][1] - cell['x_range'][0],
            cell['y_range'][1] - cell['y_range'][0],
            fill=True, facecolor='lightgreen', alpha=0.3,
            edgecolor='green', linewidth=2, linestyle='--'
        ))
    
    ax.set_xlabel(x_dim, fontsize=12, fontweight='bold')
    ax.set_ylabel(y_dim, fontsize=12, fontweight='bold')
    ax.set_title('Market Whitespace Analysis', 
                fontsize=14, fontweight='bold')
    ax.legend()
    ax.grid(True, alpha=0.3)
    
    plt.tight_layout()
    return fig, whitespace_cells

# Example usage
fig, whitespace = identify_whitespace(positioning_df, 'price_score', 'feature_score')
plt.savefig('whitespace_analysis.png', dpi=300)

print(f"\nIdentified {len(whitespace)} whitespace opportunities:")
for i, cell in enumerate(whitespace, 1):
    print(f"{i}. Position ({cell['x']:.1f}, {cell['y']:.1f})")
```

**Step 5: Multi-Dimensional Positioning Analysis**

While perceptual maps show two dimensions, competitive positioning exists across many dimensions. Conduct multi-dimensional analysis:

**Approach: Multiple Perceptual Maps**

Create several 2D maps covering different dimension pairs:
- Map 1: Price vs. Features
- Map 2: Ease of Use vs. Power
- Map 3: SMB Focus vs. Enterprise Focus
- Map 4: Innovation vs. Stability

**Approach: Radar Charts (Spider Charts)**

Compare competitors across multiple dimensions simultaneously:

```python
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import numpy as np

def create_competitive_radar(positioning_df, dimensions, companies=None):
    """
    Create radar chart comparing competitors across multiple dimensions
    
    Args:
        positioning_df: DataFrame with company positioning scores
        dimensions: List of dimension column names
        companies: List of companies to include (None = all)
    """
    
    if companies is None:
        companies = positioning_df['company'].tolist()
    
    # Filter data
    data = positioning_df[positioning_df['company'].isin(companies)]
    
    # Number of dimensions
    num_dims = len(dimensions)
    angles = np.linspace(0, 2 * np.pi, num_dims, endpoint=False).tolist()
    angles += angles[:1]  # Complete the circle
    
    # Create plot
    fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(12, 12), subplot_kw=dict(projection='polar'))
    
    colors = ['blue', 'red', 'green', 'orange', 'purple', 'brown']
    
    for idx, company in enumerate(companies):
        company_data = data[data['company'] == company]
        values = company_data[dimensions].values[0].tolist()
        values += values[:1]  # Complete the circle
        
        # Plot
        ax.plot(angles, values, 'o-', linewidth=2, 
               label=company, color=colors[idx % len(colors)])
        ax.fill(angles, values, alpha=0.15, color=colors[idx % len(colors)])
    
    # Fix axis
    ax.set_xticks(angles[:-1])
    ax.set_xticklabels(dimensions, fontsize=11)
    ax.set_ylim(0, 10)
    ax.set_yticks([2, 4, 6, 8, 10])
    ax.set_yticklabels(['2', '4', '6', '8', '10'], fontsize=9, alpha=0.6)
    ax.set_title('Multi-Dimensional Competitive Comparison', 
                fontsize=14, fontweight='bold', pad=20)
    ax.legend(loc='upper right', bbox_to_anchor=(1.3, 1.1), fontsize=10)
    ax.grid(True, alpha=0.3)
    
    plt.tight_layout()
    return fig

# Example: Multi-dimensional positioning data
multi_dim_data = pd.DataFrame({
    'company': ['Company A', 'Company B', 'Your Company', 'Company C'],
    'features': [9, 6, 8, 7],
    'ease_of_use': [6, 8, 7, 6],
    'price_value': [5, 8, 7, 6],
    'support': [8, 6, 7, 7],
    'reliability': [9, 7, 8, 6],
    'innovation': [6, 7, 8, 9]
})

dimensions = ['features', 'ease_of_use', 'price_value', 'support', 'reliability', 'innovation']

fig = create_competitive_radar(multi_dim_data, dimensions)
plt.savefig('radar_comparison.png', dpi=300, bbox_inches='tight')
plt.show()
```

**Step 6: Temporal Positioning Analysis**

Track how positioning evolves over time:

```python
def create_positioning_evolution(positioning_history, x_dim, y_dim):
    """
    Show how competitive positioning has evolved over time
    
    Args:
        positioning_history: List of DataFrames, each representing positioning at a time point
                           Each DF should have 'company', 'date', x_dim, y_dim columns
    """
    
    fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(14, 10))
    
    colors = {'Company A': 'red', 'Company B': 'blue', 
             'Your Company': 'darkgreen', 'Company C': 'orange'}
    
    for company in positioning_history[0]['company'].unique():
        # Extract company's position over time
        company_positions = []
        dates = []
        
        for period_df in positioning_history:
            company_data = period_df[period_df['company'] == company]
            if not company_data.empty:
                company_positions.append(
                    (company_data[x_dim].values[0], 
                     company_data[y_dim].values[0])
                )
                dates.append(company_data['date'].values[0])
        
        if not company_positions:
            continue
            
        # Plot trajectory
        x_vals = [pos[0] for pos in company_positions]
        y_vals = [pos[1] for pos in company_positions]
        
        color = colors.get(company, 'gray')
        
        # Plot line showing movement
        ax.plot(x_vals, y_vals, 'o-', linewidth=2, markersize=8,
               label=company, color=color, alpha=0.7)
        
        # Add arrows to show direction
        for i in range(len(x_vals)-1):
            ax.annotate('', xy=(x_vals[i+1], y_vals[i+1]),
                       xytext=(x_vals[i], y_vals[i]),
                       arrowprops=dict(arrowstyle='->', color=color, 
                                     lw=2, alpha=0.6))
        
        # Label start and end points
        ax.annotate(f"{company}\n{dates[0]}", 
                   (x_vals[0], y_vals[0]),
                   xytext=(-15, -15), textcoords='offset points',
                   fontsize=8, bbox=dict(boxstyle='round', 
                   facecolor='white', alpha=0.7, edgecolor=color))
        
        ax.annotate(f"{dates[-1]}", 
                   (x_vals[-1], y_vals[-1]),
                   xytext=(15, 15), textcoords='offset points',
                   fontsize=8, bbox=dict(boxstyle='round',
                   facecolor='white', alpha=0.7, edgecolor=color))
    
    ax.set_xlabel(x_dim, fontsize=12, fontweight='bold')
    ax.set_ylabel(y_dim, fontsize=12, fontweight='bold')
    ax.set_title('Competitive Positioning Evolution Over Time',
                fontsize=14, fontweight='bold')
    ax.legend(loc='best', fontsize=10)
    ax.grid(True, alpha=0.3)
    ax.set_xlim(0, 11)
    ax.set_ylim(0, 11)
    
    plt.tight_layout()
    return fig

# Example: Historical positioning data
positioning_2022 = pd.DataFrame({
    'company': ['Company A', 'Company B', 'Your Company'],
    'date': ['2022-01', '2022-01', '2022-01'],
    'price_score': [7, 8, 6],
    'feature_score': [8, 5, 6]
})

positioning_2023 = pd.DataFrame({
    'company': ['Company A', 'Company B', 'Your Company'],
    'date': ['2023-01', '2023-01', '2023-01'],
    'price_score': [6, 7, 7],
    'feature_score': [9, 6, 7]
})

positioning_2024 = pd.DataFrame({
    'company': ['Company A', 'Company B', 'Your Company'],
    'date': ['2024-01', '2024-01', '2024-01'],
    'price_score': [5, 6, 7],
    'feature_score': [9, 7, 8]
})

positioning_history = [positioning_2022, positioning_2023, positioning_2024]

fig = create_positioning_evolution(positioning_history, 'price_score', 'feature_score')
plt.savefig('positioning_evolution.png', dpi=300)
plt.show()
```

**Strategic Applications of Perceptual Maps:**

**1. Product Strategy:**
- Identify underserved positions
- Guide feature prioritization
- Inform pricing decisions
- Plan product line extensions

**2. Marketing Strategy:**
- Develop differentiated messaging
- Select target segments
- Position against specific competitors
- Guide brand positioning

**3. Competitive Strategy:**
- Identify direct head-to-head competitors (close proximity)
- Find flanking opportunities (whitespace)
- Assess vulnerability to new entrants
- Plan repositioning moves

**4. M&A Strategy:**
- Identify acquisition targets filling portfolio gaps
- Assess competitive overlap in potential deals
- Plan post-acquisition positioning

### 21.2 Value Curves (Strategy Canvas)

Value curves, popularized in "Blue Ocean Strategy" by Kim and Mauborgne, visualize how competitors deliver value across key competitive factors. Unlike perceptual maps (which show 2 dimensions), value curves show performance across 6-12 factors simultaneously.

**Methodology:**

**Step 1: Identify Competitive Factors**

List all factors on which companies compete in your industry. Comprehensive brainstorming yields 20-40 factors; refine to 8-12 most important.

**Example: CRM Software Competitive Factors**
1. Price (lower is better)
2. Ease of setup and onboarding
3. User interface simplicity
4. Feature comprehensiveness
5. Customization capabilities
6. Integration ecosystem
7. Mobile app quality
8. Customer support responsiveness
9. Training and education resources
10. Scalability (handles growth)
11. Security and compliance
12. Analytics and reporting

**Step 2: Score Competitors on Each Factor**

Rate each competitor on each factor using a consistent scale (e.g., 1-5 or 1-10):

**Scoring Guidelines for 1-5 Scale:**
- 1 = Well below industry standard / Not offered
- 2 = Below average / Basic offering
- 3 = Industry average / Adequate
- 4 = Above average / Strong offering
- 5 = Best in class / Exceptional

**Data Collection Methods:**

**Method A: Internal Expert Assessment**
Gather scores from your team with competitive knowledge:

```
Scoring Workshop Structure:

1. Assemble cross-functional team (Product, Sales, Marketing, CS)
2. Present list of competitive factors
3. For each factor and competitor:
   - Individual scoring (anonymous)
   - Share scores
   - Discuss discrepancies
   - Reach consensus score
4. Document rationale for each score
```

**Method B: Customer Survey**
Survey customers familiar with multiple solutions:

```
Survey Question Format:

"How would you rate [Company X] on [Factor]?"
○ 1 - Well below expectations
○ 2 - Below expectations
○ 3 - Meets expectations
○ 4 - Exceeds expectations
○ 5 - Far exceeds expectations
○ N/A - Not familiar with this aspect

Repeat for each company and factor.
```

**Method C: Objective Measurement**
For quantifiable factors, use actual data:

```python
# Example: Objective scoring for quantifiable factors

def score_price(price, industry_range):
    """
    Score price on 1-5 scale (inverted: lower price = higher score)
    """
    min_price, max_price = industry_range
    normalized = (price - min_price) / (max_price - min_price)
    # Invert: 0 (highest price) -> 1, 1 (lowest price) -> 5
    score = 5 - (normalized * 4)
    return round(score, 1)

def score_features(feature_count, industry_range):
    """
    Score feature count on 1-5 scale
    """
    min_features, max_features = industry_range
    normalized = (feature_count - min_features) / (max_features - min_features)
    score = 1 + (normalized * 4)
    return round(score, 1)

def score_integrations(integration_count, industry_range):
    """
    Score integration count on 1-5 scale
    """
    min_int, max_int = industry_range
    normalized = (integration_count - min_int) / (max_int - min_int)
    score = 1 + (normalized * 4)
    return round(score, 1)

# Example competitive data
competitors_data = {
    'Company A': {
        'price': 50,
        'features': 120,
        'integrations': 250
    },
    'Company B': {
        'price': 30,
        'features': 80,
        'integrations': 150
    },
    'Your Company': {
        'price': 40,
        'features': 100,
        'integrations': 200
    }
}

# Industry ranges (manually determined)
price_range = (20, 70)
feature_range = (50, 150)
integration_range = (50, 300)

# Calculate objective scores
for company, data in competitors_data.items():
    print(f"\n{company}:")
    print(f"  Price Score: {score_price(data['price'], price_range)}")
    print(f"  Feature Score: {score_features(data['features'], feature_range)}")
    print(f"  Integration Score: {score_integrations(data['integrations'], integration_range)}")
```

**Step 3: Create Value Curve Visualization**

Plot all competitors' value curves on a single chart:

```python
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np

def create_value_curve(value_scores_df, factors, title="Strategy Canvas"):
    """
    Create value curve (strategy canvas) visualization
    
    Args:
        value_scores_df: DataFrame with columns ['company'] + factors
                        Each row is a competitor, each column a factor score (1-5)
        factors: List of factor names (column names)
        title: Chart title
    """
    
    fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(16, 10))
    
    # Define colors and styles
    colors = {
        'Your Company': 'darkblue',
        'Company A': 'red',
        'Company B': 'green',
        'Company C': 'orange',
        'Company D': 'purple'
    }
    
    # Plot each company's value curve
    for idx, row in value_scores_df.iterrows():
        company = row['company']
        scores = row[factors].values
        
        # Determine style
        if 'Your Company' in company:
            linewidth = 4
            alpha = 1.0
            zorder = 10
            marker_size = 12
        else:
            linewidth = 2.5
            alpha = 0.7
            zorder = 5
            marker_size = 8
        
        color = colors.get(company, 'gray')
        
        # Plot line
        ax.plot(range(len(factors)), scores, 
               marker='o', markersize=marker_size,
               linewidth=linewidth, label=company,
               color=color, alpha=alpha, zorder=zorder)
        
        # Add value labels on each point
        if 'Your Company' in company:
            for i, score in enumerate(scores):
                ax.annotate(f'{score:.1f}',
                           (i, score),
                           xytext=(0, 10),
                           textcoords='offset points',
                           ha='center', fontsize=9,
                           bbox=dict(boxstyle='round,pad=0.3',
                                   facecolor='white', alpha=0.8))
    
    # Customize chart
    ax.set_xticks(range(len(factors)))
    ax.set_xticklabels(factors, rotation=45, ha='right', fontsize=11)
    ax.set_ylabel('Value Delivered (1=Low, 5=High)', 
                 fontsize=12, fontweight='bold')
    ax.set_ylim(0, 5.5)
    ax.set_yticks([1, 2, 3, 4, 5])
    ax.set_title(title, fontsize=16, fontweight='bold', pad=20)
    ax.legend(loc='upper right', fontsize=11, framealpha=0.9)
    ax.grid(True, axis='y', alpha=0.3, linestyle='--')
    ax.axhline(y=3, color='gray', linestyle=':', linewidth=1, alpha=0.5)
    
    # Add reference line labels
    ax.text(-0.5, 3, 'Industry Average', fontsize=9, 
           va='center', ha='right', style='italic', alpha=0.6)
    
    plt.tight_layout()
    return fig

# Example: Create value curve data
value_data = pd.DataFrame({
    'company': ['Your Company', 'Company A', 'Company B', 'Company C'],
    'Price Value': [3.5, 2.5, 4.5, 3.0],
    'Easy Setup': [4.0, 2.0, 3.5, 3.0],
    'UI Simplicity': [4.5, 3.0, 4.0, 3.5],
    'Features': [3.5, 5.0, 2.5, 4.0],
    'Customization': [3.0, 4.5, 2.0, 4.0],
    'Integrations': [4.0, 5.0, 3.0, 3.5],
    'Mobile App': [4.0, 3.5, 4.5, 2.5],
    'Support': [4.5, 3.0, 3.5, 3.0],
    'Training': [3.5, 4.0, 2.5, 2.0],
    'Scalability': [3.5, 5.0, 3.0, 4.5],
    'Security': [4.0, 5.0, 3.5, 4.5],
    'Analytics': [3.0, 4.5, 2.5, 4.0]
})

factors = ['Price Value', 'Easy Setup', 'UI Simplicity', 'Features', 
          'Customization', 'Integrations', 'Mobile App', 'Support',
          'Training', 'Scalability', 'Security', 'Analytics']

fig = create_value_curve(value_data, factors, 
                        title="CRM Software Strategy Canvas")
plt.savefig('value_curve.png', dpi=300, bbox_inches='tight')
plt.show()
```

**Step 4: Analyze Value Curve Insights**

Extract strategic insights from value curve patterns:

**Pattern 1: Convergence (All Curves Similar)**
- **Meaning**: Commoditized market, limited differentiation
- **Implication**: Hard to compete, price pressure
- **Strategy**: Find new factors to compete on, blue ocean opportunity

**Pattern 2: Divergence (Your Curve Different Shape)**
- **Meaning**: Differentiated strategy
- **Implication**: Clear positioning, reduced direct competition
- **Strategy**: Emphasize unique value curve in marketing

**Pattern 3: Dominated (Your Curve Below Others Everywhere)**
- **Meaning**: Competitive disadvantage across the board
- **Implication**: Need significant investment or repositioning
- **Strategy**: Focus resources on select factors, change segments

**Pattern 4: Selective Superiority (High on Some, Low on Others)**
- **Meaning**: Trade-off strategy
- **Implication**: Appeals to specific segment
- **Strategy**: Target customers who value your high factors

**Automated Pattern Analysis:**

```python
def analyze_value_curve_patterns(value_scores_df, your_company_name, factors):
    """
    Automatically analyze value curve patterns and generate insights
    """
    
    insights = []
    
    # Get your company's scores
    your_scores = value_scores_df[
        value_scores_df['company'] == your_company_name
    ][factors].values[0]
    
    # Get competitor scores
    competitor_scores = value_scores_df[
        value_scores_df['company'] != your_company_name
    ][factors].values
    
    # Calculate industry average for each factor
    industry_avg = competitor_scores.mean(axis=0)
    
    # Analysis 1: Overall Position
    your_avg = your_scores.mean()
    industry_overall_avg = industry_avg.mean()
    
    if your_avg > industry_overall_avg + 0.5:
        insights.append({
            'type': 'Strength',
            'finding': 'Overall Superior Position',
            'detail': f'Your average score ({your_avg:.2f}) exceeds industry average ({industry_overall_avg:.2f})',
            'action': 'Emphasize overall superiority in messaging'
        })
    elif your_avg < industry_overall_avg - 0.5:
        insights.append({
            'type': 'Weakness',
            'finding': 'Overall Inferior Position',
            'detail': f'Your average score ({your_avg:.2f}) below industry average ({industry_overall_avg:.2f})',
            'action': 'Focus resources on improving key factors or reposition to niche'
        })
    
    # Analysis 2: Factor-by-Factor Position
    strengths = []
    weaknesses = []
    parities = []
    
    for i, factor in enumerate(factors):
        your_score = your_scores[i]
        avg_score = industry_avg[i]
        
        diff = your_score - avg_score
        
        if diff > 0.5:
            strengths.append((factor, your_score, avg_score, diff))
        elif diff < -0.5:
            weaknesses.append((factor, your_score, avg_score, diff))
        else:
            parities.append((factor, your_score, avg_score, diff))
    
    insights.append({
        'type': 'Strengths',
        'finding': f'{len(strengths)} factors above industry average',
        'detail': ', '.join([f"{f[0]} ({f[1]:.1f} vs {f[2]:.1f})" for f in strengths[:3]]),
        'action': 'Lead with these strengths in competitive situations'
    })
    
    insights.append({
        'type': 'Weaknesses',
        'finding': f'{len(weaknesses)} factors below industry average',
        'detail': ', '.join([f"{f[0]} ({f[1]:.1f} vs {f[2]:.1f})" for f in weaknesses[:3]]),
        'action': 'Address critical weaknesses or reposition to reduce importance'
    })
    
    # Analysis 3: Differentiation Assessment
    # Calculate how different your curve is from competitors
    differentiation_scores = []
    
    for comp_scores in competitor_scores:
        diff_score = np.abs(your_scores - comp_scores).sum()
        differentiation_scores.append(diff_score)
    
    avg_differentiation = np.mean(differentiation_scores)
    
    if avg_differentiation > 8:  # Threshold for "high differentiation"
        insights.append({
            'type': 'Differentiation',
            'finding': 'Highly Differentiated Value Curve',
            'detail': f'Average difference score: {avg_differentiation:.1f}',
            'action': 'Blue ocean opportunity - emphasize unique value proposition'
        })
    elif avg_differentiation < 4:  # Threshold for "low differentiation"
        insights.append({
            'type': 'Differentiation',
            'finding': 'Commoditized Value Curve',
            'detail': f'Average difference score: {avg_differentiation:.1f}',
            'action': 'Risk of commoditization - innovate on new factors'
        })
    
    # Analysis 4: Trade-off Analysis
    # Find factors where you're high and competitors are low (and vice versa)
    trade_offs = []
    
    for i, factor in enumerate(factors):
        your_score = your_scores[i]
        comp_max = competitor_scores[:, i].max()
        comp_min = competitor_scores[:, i].min()
        
        # You're high where competitors are low
        if your_score > 4.0 and comp_max < 3.5:
            trade_offs.append({
                'factor': factor,
                'type': 'Your Strength',
                'your_score': your_score,
                'comp_range': (comp_min, comp_max)
            })
        
        # You're low where competitors are high
        elif your_score < 2.5 and comp_min > 3.5:
            trade_offs.append({
                'factor': factor,
                'type': 'Your Weakness',
                'your_score': your_score,
                'comp_range': (comp_min, comp_max)
            })
    
    if trade_offs:
        insights.append({
            'type': 'Trade-offs',
            'finding': f'{len(trade_offs)} strategic trade-offs detected',
            'detail': ', '.join([f"{t['factor']} ({t['type']})" for t in trade_offs[:3]]),
            'action': 'Ensure trade-offs align with target segment priorities'
        })
    
    return pd.DataFrame(insights)

# Example usage
insights_df = analyze_value_curve_patterns(value_data, 'Your Company', factors)
print("\n=== VALUE CURVE STRATEGIC INSIGHTS ===\n")
for idx, row in insights_df.iterrows():
    print(f"{row['type']}: {row['finding']}")
    print(f"  Detail: {row['detail']}")
    print(f"  Action: {row['action']}\n")
```

**Step 5: Blue Ocean Strategy Analysis**

Use value curves to identify "blue ocean" opportunities - uncontested market spaces:

**Four Actions Framework:**

Apply the four actions framework to create a new value curve:

1. **ELIMINATE**: Which factors the industry competes on should be eliminated?
2. **REDUCE**: Which factors should be reduced well below industry standard?
3. **RAISE**: Which factors should be raised well above industry standard?
4. **CREATE**: Which factors should be created that the industry has never offered?

```python
def generate_blue_ocean_opportunities(value_data, your_company, factors):
    """
    Generate blue ocean strategy recommendations using Four Actions Framework
    """
    
    # Calculate industry average for each factor
    competitors_data = value_data[value_data['company'] != your_company]
    industry_avg = competitors_data[factors].mean()
    
    your_scores = value_data[value_data['company'] == your_company][factors].values[0]
    
    opportunities = {
        'eliminate': [],
        'reduce': [],
        'raise': [],
        'create': []
    }
    
    # ELIMINATE: Factors where everyone scores 3+, little differentiation, high cost
    # (Requires manual judgment, but can identify candidates)
    for i, factor in enumerate(factors):
        avg = industry_avg[i]
        std = competitors_data[factors].std()[i]
        
        # Low variation + high scores = potential elimination candidate
        if std < 0.5 and avg >= 3.5:
            opportunities['eliminate'].append({
                'factor': factor,
                'reason': f'Low differentiation (std={std:.2f}), high baseline ({avg:.2f})',
                'recommendation': f'Consider eliminating {factor} if costly to maintain'
            })
    
    # REDUCE: Factors where industry over-delivers relative to customer needs
    # (Requires customer research, but can identify candidates)
    for i, factor in enumerate(factors):
        avg = industry_avg[i]
        
        # High industry average suggests potential over-delivery
        if avg >= 4.0:
            opportunities['reduce'].append({
                'factor': factor,
                'reason': f'Industry average very high ({avg:.2f}), possible over-delivery',
                'recommendation': f'Validate if customers value {factor} at current level. If not, reduce to save cost.'
            })
    
    # RAISE: Factors where you're average/below but could differentiate
    for i, factor in enumerate(factors):
        your_score = your_scores[i]
        avg = industry_avg[i]
        std = competitors_data[factors].std()[i]
        
        # You're at/below average, but there's variation (room for differentiation)
        if your_score <= avg and std > 0.5:
            opportunities['raise'].append({
                'factor': factor,
                'reason': f'Your score ({your_score:.1f}) at/below average ({avg:.2f}), differentiation opportunity',
                'recommendation': f'Invest in significantly raising {factor} above industry standard'
            })
    
    # CREATE: (Requires innovation brainstorming - provide framework)
    opportunities['create'].append({
        'factor': 'New Factor Ideas',
        'reason': 'No competitors compete on these factors',
        'recommendation': 'Brainstorm: What do customers value that no one delivers? Emerging needs? Adjacent industry factors to import?'
    })
    
    return opportunities

# Example usage
blue_ocean_opps = generate_blue_ocean_opportunities(value_data, 'Your Company', factors)

print("\n=== BLUE OCEAN STRATEGY OPPORTUNITIES ===\n")

print("ELIMINATE (Reduce cost, remove unnecessary factors):")
for opp in blue_ocean_opps['eliminate'][:3]:
    print(f"  • {opp['factor']}")
    print(f"    {opp['recommendation']}\n")

print("\nREDUCE (Lower to industry minimum, save cost):")
for opp in blue_ocean_opps['reduce'][:3]:
    print(f"  • {opp['factor']}")
    print(f"    {opp['recommendation']}\n")

print("\nRAISE (Invest to exceed industry):")
for opp in blue_ocean_opps['raise'][:3]:
    print(f"  • {opp['factor']}")
    print(f"    {opp['recommendation']}\n")

print("\nCREATE (Introduce new competitive factors):")
print(f"  • {blue_ocean_opps['create'][0]['recommendation']}")
```

**Step 6: Segment-Specific Value Curves**

Different customer segments may value factors differently. Create segment-specific value curves:

```python
def create_segment_value_curves(value_data, factors, segments_info):
    """
    Create value curves for different customer segments
    
    Args:
        value_data: DataFrame with company scores on each factor
        factors: List of factors
        segments_info: Dict mapping segment names to factor weights
                      Example: {'Enterprise': {'Features': 1.5, 'Support': 1.3, ...}, ...}
    """
    
    fig, axes = plt.subplots(1, len(segments_info), figsize=(8*len(segments_info), 8))
    
    if len(segments_info) == 1:
        axes = [axes]
    
    for idx, (segment_name, weights) in enumerate(segments_info.items()):
        ax = axes[idx]
        
        # Apply segment-specific weights to scores
        weighted_data = value_data.copy()
        
        for factor in factors:
            weight = weights.get(factor, 1.0)  # Default weight = 1.0
            weighted_data[factor] = weighted_data[factor] * weight
        
        # Plot value curves for this segment
        for _, row in weighted_data.iterrows():
            company = row['company']
            scores = row[factors].values
            
            linewidth = 4 if 'Your Company' in company else 2.5
            alpha = 1.0 if 'Your Company' in company else 0.7
            
            ax.plot(range(len(factors)), scores,
                   marker='o', markersize=8, linewidth=linewidth,
                   label=company, alpha=alpha)
        
        ax.set_xticks(range(len(factors)))
        ax.set_xticklabels(factors, rotation=45, ha='right', fontsize=9)
        ax.set_ylabel('Weighted Value', fontsize=11, fontweight='bold')
        ax.set_title(f'{segment_name} Segment Value Curve',
                    fontsize=13, fontweight='bold')
        ax.legend(loc='upper right', fontsize=9)
        ax.grid(True, axis='y', alpha=0.3)
    
    plt.tight_layout()
    return fig

# Example: Segment-specific weights
segment_weights = {
    'SMB': {
        'Price Value': 1.5,      # SMBs care more about price
        'Easy Setup': 1.4,       # Want quick setup
        'UI Simplicity': 1.3,    # Need simplicity
        'Features': 0.8,         # Don't need all features
        'Scalability': 0.7,      # Less concerned about scale
        'Security': 0.9          # Lower security requirements
    },
    'Enterprise': {
        'Price Value': 0.8,      # Less price sensitive
        'Easy Setup': 0.9,       # Have resources for complex setup
        'UI Simplicity': 0.8,    # Can handle complexity
        'Features': 1.4,         # Need comprehensive features
        'Customization': 1.5,    # Need customization
        'Scalability': 1.5,      # Critical need
        'Security': 1.5,         # Critical need
        'Integrations': 1.4      # Need many integrations
    }
}

fig = create_segment_value_curves(value_data, factors, segment_weights)
plt.savefig('segment_value_curves.png', dpi=300, bbox_inches='tight')
plt.show()
```

**Strategic Applications of Value Curves:**

1. **Product Strategy**:
   - Identify which factors to invest in (raise/create)
   - Identify which factors to reduce/eliminate (cost savings)
   - Guide product roadmap prioritization

2. **Marketing Strategy**:
   - Visualize differentiation for sales collateral
   - Create clear positioning messages
   - Develop segment-specific value propositions

3. **Pricing Strategy**:
   - Justify premium pricing (superior value curve)
   - Justify value pricing (balanced value curve)
   - Explain trade-offs (selective superiority)

4. **M&A Strategy**:
   - Identify acquisition targets that complement your curve
   - Assess competitive threats from acquisitions
   - Plan post-merger positioning

### 21.3 Feature Matrices and Comparison Tables

Feature matrices provide detailed, attribute-level competitive comparisons. Unlike high-level positioning tools, feature matrices offer granular product capability intelligence for buyers evaluating solutions.

**Methodology:**

**Step 1: Feature Taxonomy Development**

Create a comprehensive, hierarchical feature taxonomy:

**Example: CRM Feature Taxonomy**

```
Level 1: Major Categories
├── Contact Management
├── Sales Pipeline Management
├── Marketing Automation
├── Customer Service
├── Analytics & Reporting
├── Integrations
├── Mobile
├── Administration

Level 2: Feature Groups (within each category)
Contact Management:
├── Contact Records
├── Company Records
├── Contact Segmentation
├── Contact Enrichment
├── Duplicate Management

Level 3: Specific Features (within each group)
Contact Records:
├── Custom fields
├── Contact history timeline
├── Contact tags
├── Contact notes
├── Contact attachments
├── Contact relationships
├── Contact social profiles
```

**Full Taxonomy Extraction Methods:**

**Method A: Aggregate Competitor Feature Lists**

1. Visit each competitor's features page
2. Extract all listed features
3. Organize into common categories
4. Merge overlapping features
5. Create unified taxonomy

```python
import requests
from bs4 import BeautifulSoup
import pandas as pd

def scrape_features_page(url):
    """
    Scrape features from a competitor's feature page
    (Note: Respect robots.txt and terms of service)
    """
    try:
        response = requests.get(url, headers={'User-Agent': 'Mozilla/5.0'})
        soup = BeautifulSoup(response.content, 'html.parser')
        
        # Extract features (adjust selectors for each site)
        features = []
        
        # Common patterns:
        # Pattern 1: Features in <li> tags within <ul class="features">
        feature_lists = soup.find_all('ul', class_='features')
        for ul in feature_lists:
            for li in ul.find_all('li'):
                features.append(li.get_text().strip())
        
        # Pattern 2: Features in <div class="feature-item">
        feature_divs = soup.find_all('div', class_='feature-item')
        for div in feature_divs:
            title = div.find('h3') or div.find('h4')
            if title:
                features.append(title.get_text().strip())
        
        # Pattern 3: Features in table rows
        feature_table = soup.find('table', class_='feature-table')
        if feature_table:
            for row in feature_table.find_all('tr'):
                cells = row.find_all('td')
                if cells:
                    features.append(cells[0].get_text().strip())
        
        return features
    
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Error scraping {url}: {e}")
        return []

# Example usage:
competitors = {
    'Company A': 'https://companya.com/features',
    'Company B': 'https://companyb.com/features',
    'Company C': 'https://companyc.com/product'
}

all_features = {}

for company, url in competitors.items():
    print(f"Scraping {company}...")
    features = scrape_features_page(url)
    all_features[company] = features
    print(f"  Found {len(features)} features")

# Merge into unified feature list
unique_features = set()
for features_list in all_features.values():
    unique_features.update(features_list)

print(f"\nTotal unique features across all competitors: {len(unique_features)}")
```

**Method B: Review Site Feature Lists**

G2, Capterra, and similar sites maintain comprehensive feature taxonomies. Export these:

1. Visit competitor profile on G2/Capterra
2. Navigate to "Features" section
3. Export or scrape feature list with checkmarks
4. Aggregate across competitors

**Method C: Manual Documentation Review**

Deep review of:
- Product documentation
- Help center articles
- API documentation
- Admin guides
- Training materials

**Step 2: Feature Matrix Data Collection**

For each feature and competitor, determine:
- ✓ Yes (feature exists)
- ✗ No (feature doesn't exist)
- ⊕ Partial (limited implementation)
- $ Paid add-on (not included in base)
- ? Unknown (couldn't determine)

**Data Collection Template:**

```python
import pandas as pd

# Feature matrix data structure
feature_matrix = {
    'Feature': [],
    'Category': [],
    'Your Company': [],
    'Competitor A': [],
    'Competitor B': [],
    'Competitor C': [],
    'Notes': []
}

# Example population:
features_data = [
    {
        'Feature': 'Custom Fields',
        'Category': 'Contact Management',
        'Your Company': '✓',
        'Competitor A': '✓',
        'Competitor B': '✓',
        'Competitor C': '⊕',
        'Notes': 'Competitor C: Limited to 10 custom fields'
    },
    {
        'Feature': 'Email Sequences',
        'Category': 'Marketing Automation',
        'Your Company': '✓',
        'Competitor A': '$',
        'Competitor B': '✓',
        'Competitor C': '✗',
        'Notes': 'Competitor A: Requires Professional plan'
    },
    {
        'Feature': 'Visual Workflow Builder',
        'Category': 'Marketing Automation',
        'Your Company': '✓',
        'Competitor A': '✓',
        'Competitor B': '⊕',
        'Competitor C': '✗',
        'Notes': 'Competitor B: Basic builder, no conditional logic'
    },
    # ... hundreds more features
]

# Save to CSV

```

**Automated Feature Detection:**

Some features can be detected automatically:

```python
def detect_integrations(company_domain):
    """
    Detect integrations by scanning integrations page
    """
    integrations_urls = [
        f"https://{company_domain}/integrations",
        f"https://{company_domain}/marketplace",
        f"https://{company_domain}/apps"
    ]
    
    integrations = []
    
    for url in integrations_urls:
        try:
            response = requests.get(url, timeout=10, 
                                   headers={'User-Agent': 'Mozilla/5.0'})
            if response.status_code == 200:
                soup = BeautifulSoup(response.content, 'html.parser')
                
                # Look for integration names/logos
                # Pattern 1: Integration cards
                int_cards = soup.find_all(['div', 'a'], class_=lambda x: x and 'integration' in x.lower())
                
                for card in int_cards:
                    name_elem = card.find(['h3', 'h4', 'span', 'div'])
                    if name_elem:
                        integrations.append(name_elem.get_text().strip())
                
                # Pattern 2: Lists
                int_lists = soup.find_all(['ul', 'ol'], class_=lambda x: x and 'integration' in x.lower())
                for ul in int_lists:
                    for li in ul.find_all('li'):
                        integrations.append(li.get_text().strip())
                
                break  # Found the page
        
        except:
            continue
    
    return list(set(integrations))  # Remove duplicates

def detect_pricing_tiers(company_domain):
    """
    Detect pricing tiers and what's included
    """
    pricing_urls = [
        f"https://{company_domain}/pricing",
        f"https://{company_domain}/plans"
    ]
    
    tiers = []
    
    for url in pricing_urls:
        try:
            response = requests.get(url, timeout=10,
                                   headers={'User-Agent': 'Mozilla/5.0'})
            if response.status_code == 200:
                soup = BeautifulSoup(response.content, 'html.parser')
                
                # Look for pricing cards/boxes
                price_cards = soup.find_all(['div', 'section'], 
                                           class_=lambda x: x and any(term in x.lower() 
                                           for term in ['plan', 'tier', 'pricing']))
                
                for card in price_cards:
                    tier = {}
                    
                    # Extract plan name
                    name_elem = card.find(['h2', 'h3', 'h4'])
                    if name_elem:
                        tier['name'] = name_elem.get_text().strip()
                    
                    # Extract price
                    price_elem = card.find(class_=lambda x: x and 'price' in x.lower())
                    if price_elem:
                        tier['price'] = price_elem.get_text().strip()
                    
                    # Extract features
                    feature_list = card.find(['ul', 'ol'])
                    if feature_list:
                        tier['features'] = [li.get_text().strip() 
                                          for li in feature_list.find_all('li')]
                    
                    if tier:
                        tiers.append(tier)
                
                break
        
        except:
            continue
    
    return tiers

# Example usage
company = "hubspot.com"
integrations = detect_integrations(company)
print(f"Detected {len(integrations)} integrations for {company}")

tiers = detect_pricing_tiers(company)
print(f"Detected {len(tiers)} pricing tiers")
for tier in tiers:
    print(f"  {tier.get('name', 'Unknown')}: {tier.get('price', 'N/A')}")
```

**Step 3: Feature Matrix Analysis**

Analyze the completed feature matrix for strategic insights:

**Gap Analysis:**

```python
def analyze_feature_gaps(feature_matrix_df):
    """
    Analyze feature gaps vs. competitors
    """
    your_company_col = 'Your Company'
    competitor_cols = [col for col in feature_matrix_df.columns 
                      if col not in ['Feature', 'Category', your_company_col, 'Notes']]
    
    gaps = {
        'missing': [],      # You don't have, competitors do
        'unique': [],       # You have, competitors don't
        'partial': [],      # You have partial, competitors have full
        'advantages': []    # You have, most competitors don't
    }
    
    for idx, row in feature_matrix_df.iterrows():
        feature = row['Feature']
        category = row['Category']
        your_status = row[your_company_col]
        
        competitor_statuses = [row[col] for col in competitor_cols]
        
        # Count how many competitors have this feature
        competitor_have = sum([1 for s in competitor_statuses if s == '✓'])
        total_competitors = len(competitor_cols)
        
        # Missing: You don't have, but 50%+ competitors do
        if your_status in ['✗', '?'] and competitor_have >= total_competitors / 2:
            gaps['missing'].append({
                'feature': feature,
                'category': category,
                'competitor_coverage': f"{competitor_have}/{total_competitors}"
            })
        
        # Unique: You have, but <25% of competitors do
        if your_status == '✓' and competitor_have < total_competitors / 4:
            gaps['unique'].append({
                'feature': feature,
                'category': category,
                'competitor_coverage': f"{competitor_have}/{total_competitors}"
            })
        
        # Partial: You have partial, others have full
        if your_status == '⊕' and competitor_have >= 2:
            gaps['partial'].append({
                'feature': feature,
                'category': category,
                'your_status': 'Partial',
                'competitors_with_full': competitor_have
            })
        
        # Advantage: You have full, most competitors don't or have partial
        competitor_full = sum([1 for s in competitor_statuses if s == '✓'])
        if your_status == '✓' and competitor_full <= 1:
            gaps['advantages'].append({
                'feature': feature,
                'category': category,
                'competitors_with_full': competitor_full
            })
    
    return gaps

# Run analysis
gaps = analyze_feature_gaps(df)

print(f"\n=== FEATURE GAP ANALYSIS ===\n")
print(f"Missing Features (Competitive Gaps): {len(gaps['missing'])}")
for gap in gaps['missing'][:10]:  # Top 10
    print(f"  • {gap['feature']} ({gap['category']}) - {gap['competitor_coverage']} competitors have it")

print(f"\nUnique Features (Differentiators): {len(gaps['unique'])}")
for gap in gaps['unique'][:10]:
    print(f"  • {gap['feature']} ({gap['category']}) - Only you have it")

print(f"\nPartial Implementations: {len(gaps['partial'])}")
for gap in gaps['partial'][:10]:
    print(f"  • {gap['feature']} ({gap['category']}) - You have partial, {gap['competitors_with_full']} have full")

print(f"\nCompetitive Advantages: {len(gaps['advantages'])}")
for gap in gaps['advantages'][:10]:
    print(f"  • {gap['feature']} ({gap['category']}) - You have full, only {gap['competitors_with_full']} competitors do")
```

**Coverage Analysis by Category:**

```python
def analyze_category_coverage(feature_matrix_df):
    """
    Calculate feature coverage by category for each competitor
    """
    categories = feature_matrix_df['Category'].unique()
    
    company_cols = [col for col in feature_matrix_df.columns 
                   if col not in ['Feature', 'Category', 'Notes']]
    
    coverage = []
    
    for category in categories:
        category_features = feature_matrix_df[feature_matrix_df['Category'] == category]
        total_features = len(category_features)
        
        row = {'Category': category, 'Total Features': total_features}
        
        for company in company_cols:
            # Count features this company has
            has_full = sum(category_features[company] == '✓')
            has_partial = sum(category_features[company] == '⊕')
            has_paid = sum(category_features[company] == '$')
            
            coverage_pct = (has_full + 0.5*has_partial) / total_features * 100
            
            row[company] = coverage_pct
            row[f"{company}_detail"] = f"{has_full}✓ {has_partial}⊕ {has_paid}$"
        
        coverage.append(row)
    
    coverage_df = pd.DataFrame(coverage)
    return coverage_df

# Run analysis
coverage_df = analyze_category_coverage(df)

print("\n=== CATEGORY COVERAGE ANALYSIS ===\n")
print(coverage_df.to_string(index=False))

# Visualize coverage
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import numpy as np

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(14, 8))

categories = coverage_df['Category']
companies = [col for col in coverage_df.columns 
            if col not in ['Category', 'Total Features'] and '_detail' not in col]

x = np.arange(len(categories))
width = 0.8 / len(companies)

for i, company in enumerate(companies):
    offset = (i - len(companies)/2) * width + width/2
    bars = ax.bar(x + offset, coverage_df[company], width, label=company)

ax.set_xlabel('Category', fontsize=12, fontweight='bold')
ax.set_ylabel('Coverage %', fontsize=12, fontweight='bold')
ax.set_title('Feature Coverage by Category', fontsize=14, fontweight='bold')
ax.set_xticks(x)
ax.set_xticklabels(categories, rotation=45, ha='right')
ax.legend()
ax.set_ylim(0, 110)
ax.grid(True, axis='y', alpha=0.3)

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('category_coverage.png', dpi=300)
plt.show()
```

**Step 4: Public-Facing Feature Comparison Pages**

Create customer-facing feature comparison matrices:

def generate_feature_comparison_html(feature_matrix_df, output_file='comparison.html'):
